Since mid-2005, the two supreme audit institutions have been discussing close cooperation in the control of the construction of the motorway connecting Prague and Dresden.
Both sides had serious reasons for such cooperation:
1.
The SRN is financially involved in the construction of the border bridge.
Its planning and construction is based on the RMD.
Therefore, BRH itself did not have the opportunity to carry out the control of this construction project.
2.
The SAO did not have the comparative database needed to assess whether the funds were used in a cost-effective manner with regard to the overall level of the costs of bridges and tunnels on D 8.
3.
The SAO needed to make sure that the expenditures were effective with regard to the main technical parameters of the motorway, especially the arrangement of bridges and tunnels, which should allow for the same quality of travel on both sides of the border.
After the preparatory negotiations, the Agreement on the Coordinated Inspection of the D 8/A motorway 17 was signed by the presidents of both supreme audit institutions on 4 January 2006.
The joint working meetings of the auditors of BRH and the SAO took place at an interval of approximately six to eight weeks throughout 2006.
The meetings dealt with current problems and tasks, in particular the preparation of a uniform methodology for the control of the prices of bridges and tunnels, the exchange of findings from the inspection of the construction of the border bridge and the preparation of joint reports.
An important sign of cooperation was the continuous exchange of information.
While BRH carried out most of the work on the comparison of technical parameters of bridges and tunnels and the tendering procedure, the SAO prepared for the most part the methodology for the control of the prices of bridges and tunnels.
The applicable legal standards for control in the state administration differ in the way the auditing operations are concluded in both countries.
For these reasons, the two supreme audit institutions drew their own audit conclusions on their findings obtained during their audits.
This report reflects the results achieved during the parallel audits.
The cooperation will be concluded by the signing of the final communiqué in the first half of 2007.
Construction of the Border Bridge
The Czech Republic and the Federal Republic of Germany agreed in a contract from 2000 on a date of completion of the construction of the border bridge in 2005, if possible.
Both parties then agreed to postpone this date until the end of 2006.
The RMD acted as an investor.
The SRN contributes financially to the construction of the bridge from 42.13%.
In the period up to 1999, the RMD proposed that the border bridge should have a prestressed reinforced concrete structure.
In 1999, in agreement with its partners in Germany, the RMD agreed on a coupled steel-concrete structure.
This decision was justified by the reduction of the number of double pillars from seven to five and the consequent environmental benefits of such a solution for the valley.
Moreover, comparative calculations carried out by the RMD indicated that the combined steel-concrete structure should be a less costly option.
However, there was an error in the calculations.
It was stated that the new combined steel-concrete structure would be only 397 m long.
In fact, it is 5% longer.
During the award of the border bridge contract, the RMD made serious errors.
It did not prepare the contract documentation adequately describing the assessment criteria and sub-criteria for comparing the bids.
This error led to the evaluation committee and the general manager evaluating the bids in a subjective and problematic manner.
The unsuccessful bidder challenged the results of the contract procedure.
A subsequent investigation by the Office for the Protection of Competition led to the contract for the construction being signed later than originally calculated.
This meant that the 2004 summer construction season, after the evaluation of the bids, was wasted and the construction period shortened.
The construction company agreed to build the bridge in a shorter time, but at the cost of a substantial increase in costs.
The procedure offered included the double assembly of the bridge superstructure, a change in the structure of the pillars and the installation and fitting-out of the assembly area.
Moreover, the construction company was not able to procure the type of steel originally proposed in due time.
The new type of steel – at a higher price of about €4 million, which had to be fully paid by the RMD – allowed for a shortening of the time to perform corrosion protection, which in turn allowed for a shortening of the total construction period of the border bridge.
Compared to the net price of €18.3 million offered in the tender, the total cost of building the border bridge will increase considerably due to the accelerated procedure and other modifications in the financing.
The shortened construction period led to a price increase of about €5.9 million.
Currently, the net cost amount is around €25.9 million, not taking into account the automatic price increase (as of October 2006).
Comparison of bridge and tunnel prices
The two supreme audit institutions carried out an analysis of bridge prices on the D 8 and A 17 motorways in order to identify bridges whose price was too high and to compare bridge prices on the German and Czech sides.
The analysis was based on methods of mathematical statistics.
Furthermore, the tunnels on the D 8 were compared with similar tunnels on the motorways in Germany (A 71, A 38 and A 4).
There are no similar tunnels on the A 17.
In preparing this part of the inspection, it was important to identify the relevant prices accurately.
The two supreme audit institutions set the following principles for comparing bridge prices: to base the actual price paid for the bridge excluding value added tax (VAT rates differ in the Czech Republic and the SNR and are subject to changes); not to take into account the prices for the special foundations of the bridge; to convert the prices in CZK into € according to the European Central Bank rate in force on 30.12.2005 (CZK 29 = € 1).
On both D 8 and A 17 there are bridges of different constructions (steel-concrete, prestressed reinforced concrete, combined steel-concrete and steel) and different sizes.
In order to ensure the homogeneity of the constructions examined, the bridges were divided into groups according to the type of construction.
Within these groups, the size of the bridge was also taken into account in the analyses.
Concrete bridges on both sides of the border were by far the most common type of construction (20 bridges on D 8, 36 bridges on A 17).
The analysis focused mainly on this group of bridges, as the number of bridges allowed the use of mathematical-statistical methods with a high degree of reliability of the results.
The inspections found that the prices per m 2 of concrete bridges on both D 8 and A 17 depended on the size of the bridge (its length, area): the larger the bridge, the lower the price per m2 of the bridge structure.
In the case of German bridges, the dependence of the price on the size was stronger than in the case of Czech bridges (see the graphs below).
Two concrete bridges on A 17 and four on D 8 were identified, the price of which is significantly higher.
In order to find these bridges, a method was used to identify atypical values in a set of numbers, on the one hand, and a regression analysis taking into account the dependence of the price of the bridge on its size (the distance from the regression curve upwards is decisive for the determination of atypically expensive bridges).
However, information on the atypical high price of these bridges should be considered only as an indicator of which bridges should be targeted.
In order to draw a generally applicable conclusion, the causes of the high price in each identified case should be analysed.
One such cause may be inefficient administrative measures, or also environmental protection requirements or other plausible reason.
Considering that Czech concrete bridges are on average longer than German ones (see table below), the proven negative dependence of the price of the bridge on its length could lead to the assumption that Czech bridges should be cheaper.
The hypothesis of lower prices of Czech concrete bridges is also supported by lower labour costs in the construction industry in the Czech Republic compared to Germany.
On the contrary, the results of the analysis show that the prices of concrete bridges on D 8 are higher than on A 17.
During the joint negotiations, two possible causes of this state of affairs were identified:
1.
In Germany, most bridges are awarded as separate contracts.
Such an award allows small and medium-sized regional firms to participate in the tender.
In the Czech Republic, many bridges are awarded as part of the contract for a part of the motorway route, which is several kilometres long.
Small and medium-sized firms do not participate in such a tender because the contract is too big for them.
2.
In the Czech Republic, there is more variability in the construction of bridges than in Germany.
In this context, the SAO found that the type of bridge construction is decided long before the bridge is built.
The decision is taken at the time when the SAO submits the application for a zoning decision.
At this time, the SAD selects types of bridges from several variants, but economy plays a smaller role in the decision.
A general conclusion on the prices of bridges in the Czech Republic and Germany could be reached only on the basis of a thorough analysis of the construction works market in both countries, which would go beyond this control action.
The border bridge is the most expensive coupled steel concrete bridge on D 8 and A 17.
In the case of tunnels, the prices of tunnels on D 8 were found to be twice as high as those in Germany.
However, a comparison of the main construction-technical parameters of tunnels on D 8 with those in Germany did not show any substantial difference.
Significant price differences could be attributed to the fact that there is no real competition in the tunnel construction sector in the Czech Republic.
However, the small number of tunnels did not allow for generally applicable conclusions.
Effectiveness and efficiency of the Dresden-Prague motorway construction
The independent Czech Republic was established in 1993.
The government approved the concept of the motorway network development in the same year.
The D 8 motorway was to be gradually extended from Prague to the state border with Germany.
Section No 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains was to be acquired before the border section No 0807.
The Czech Republic acquires the D 8 motorway as part of the programme financing.
The programme No 327 220 "Construction of the D 8 motorway Prague – Ústí nad Labem – state border of the Czech Republic/Germany" set two objectives for the construction of the D 8 motorway:
1.
to improve the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem – a national objective;
2.
to improve the connection between Prague and Berlin within the framework of the IV Trans-European Transport Corridor Berlin – Istanbul – an international objective.
According to the programme, the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Highlands was to be completed in June 2008 and the construction of the border section No 0807 in June 2007.
It should be added that the completion date is not identical to the date of commissioning.
The SAO found that the preparation of the construction of the section leading through the Czech Central Highlands is now at the stage of an official zoning decision.
Environmental activists challenged this decision with legal actions.
The legal action is not final.
It cannot therefore be excluded that the section leading through the Czech Central Highlands will be completed several years later than indicated in the programme documentation.
In the summer of 2006, the Czech Ministry of Transport proposed to postpone the completion date of the section No 0805 to 2010.
As a result of the above mentioned facts, the construction of the border section did not achieve the national goal of improving the connection between Prague and Ústí nad Labem.
The RMD carried out an estimate of capital expenditures for the border section D 8 No 0807 and section No 0805 through the Czech Central Mountains in the years 1999-2001.
The estimate of the capital expenditures included in these capital expenditures amounts to about 639 million € for both sections No 0805 and No 0807 together.
Table 2: Results of the economic assessment (EIRR = internal rate of profitability)
All considered motorways and expressways opened, both sections D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section traffic intensity of 18,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All considered motorways and expressways opened, both sections D 8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section traffic intensity of 22,000 vehicles per day in 2010
All motorways and express roads under consideration opened, both sections of D8 opened in 2007.
On the northern part of the border section, the traffic intensity of 25 000 vehicles per day in 2010
The economic analysis carried out in 2001 was based on the assumption that the border section and the section through the Bohemian Central Highlands would be completed on time.
However, at the time of the inspection, it is clear that the section through the Bohemian Central Highlands will not be opened at the original date.
This will reduce the above mentioned EIRR values.
The cost of both sections, net of VAT, calculated as at 31.12.2005, was €897 million, i.e. higher by 27.4% than the cost level worked out by the economic analysis in 2001.
However, there is a risk that section 0805 will be run on a different, more expensive route, which would affect the above mentioned economic viability threshold.
The economic analysis showed that the construction of both sections of the motorway is economically viable at a traffic intensity of at least 18 000 vehicles per day in 2010.
The forecast for 2008 was 22,940 vehicles, but the study dealing with the issue of bypass routes for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains calculates traffic intensity of 12,600 to 13,750 vehicles per day, which pushes the investment below the limit of economic viability.
With a higher load on bypass routes for the missing section through the Czech Central Mountains, there is a high probability of congestion and thus an increase in external costs, which were not foreseen in the original economic analysis.
The above-mentioned decrease in revenues and increase in costs means that the already low EIRR that the construction of the border section D 8 should have had will not be achieved.
Construction of the D 8 border section is therefore inefficient.
Also during the construction of the A 17 motorway, there were similar cost overruns as in the case of these two sections on D 8.
As part of the overall economic assessment of the federal road development plan for long-distance transport for 1992, the Federal Ministry of Transport of Germany projected costs of about 320 million €.
The resulting cost/benefit ratio was then 4.6.
In the meantime, however, the cost had risen sharply to about €670 million and the cost-benefit ratio had been more than halved.
In addition to the parallel inspection carried out jointly with the SAO, the BRH audited the preparation and construction of the A 17 motorway in 2003 and 2005.
The BRH found that the competent state approval authority had requested an off-road tunnel for the third section of the A 17, which was not needed.
During the auditing operation, the BRH managed to convince the Federal Ministry and the German Road Construction Administration that the tunnel was not necessary.
The result was savings, which amounted to about €10 million in construction costs alone and €0.15 million in annual operating costs.
When assessing the first section of the A 17, the BRH found that the construction project had not been properly prepared.
This is illustrated by the following example:
In the preparatory stage, the Road Construction Administration did not carry out a survey of the bedrock conditions in the Dölzschen rocks, as the site was difficult to access.
This was carried out only after the tunnel digging machine had hit the rock.
Tests showed that the rocks were more weathered inside than anticipated during the tender.
Therefore, costly measures such as anchoring, injection and safety nets against falling rocks had to be taken, which cost about €3.3 million.
The Road and Motorway Directorate had to bear the costs not only of other emergency operations such as safety measures in the Dölzschen rocks, but also of measures that would not have been necessary if the construction project had been properly prepared first.
Expenditure of this kind includes costs related to interruption of construction operations (€3.2 million), additional training of drilling staff (€0.5 million) and increasing wages and expenditure on goods due to the project's exceeding duration (€7 million).
The cost overruns thus amounted to about €10.7 million, which did not correspond to any added value.
Based on the lessons learned from this and other audited projects, BRH asked the Federal Ministry to apply stricter guidance in its supervision of road construction works.
The Federal Ministry should give the road and motorway directorates the necessary impetus to implement quality assurance systems.
Benefits of cooperation for BRH and the SAO The cooperation fulfilled the intention of both auditing institutions.
BRH gained audit knowledge about the preparation, assignment and progress of the construction of the border bridge.
Both supreme auditing institutions built a database of bridge and tunnel prices on which future audits could be based.
The SAO found that the main construction and technical parameters of bridges, tunnels and the route of the D 8 motorway did not differ from the parameters of the A 17.
Auditors of both institutions involved in the cooperation gained new experience which can be used in further audits.

Coordinated control of funds earmarked for financing projects implemented under the Cross-Border Cooperation Operational Programme Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007-2013
INTRODUCTION
The developing community of Europe's population is a driving force behind cross-border cooperation as an integral part of the wider territorial cooperation that has been part of our lives for the last 20 years.
So let this community give an introduction to this joint report by the two supreme audit institutions of neighbouring countries:
"185 million EU citizens (or 37% of the population) live in border areas.
These areas are usually underdeveloped and peripheral territories, often a relic of the darker parts of Europe's history.
The main objective of cross-border cooperation is to help alleviate the existence of borders as administrative, legal and physical barriers and further help develop the dormant potential of border regions.
The programmes cover different border regions: some cover maritime areas, others inland borders within the EU or borders shared with candidate countries.
Most programmes are bilateral, but some also cover more than two neighbouring countries."
The programmes run for seven years and receive a total of €5.7 billion from the EU budget.
"The programmes include the following activities: repairs and reconstruction of cross-border roads, cycle paths and bridges, investment in cross-border waste management systems, medical facilities, research centres, flood control measures, etc., joint management of natural sites or development of tourist destinations, development of common services for local populations, advice on employment issues, creation of thematic networks and clusters for innovation.
Support from the programme is provided on the condition that organisations from both sides of the border (e.g. regional authorities, universities or SMEs) come together to carry out projects based on the needs of the border region.
Representatives of the cooperating regions, relevant ministries and other local partners meet regularly and decide which projects are to be supported.
This system should lead to a sustainable form of cooperation even after the programme itself ends.
Funding is provided on the condition that project partners from both sides of the border work together in a way that fulfils at least two of the following four characteristics: joint preparation, joint implementation, joint staff, joint financing.’
Within the partnership, the tasks and responsibilities for the preparation, implementation, financing and control of project activities are clearly defined and distributed to the relevant partners.
The main attributes of the partnership are: joint choice of the lead partner, joint project preparation and applications, conclusion of a joint partnership agreement signed by all partners, assumption of overall responsibility for project implementation by the lead partner, total payments of support are received by the lead partner and transferred to the respective project partners.
Eligible projects implemented on the territory of the regions and voivodships adjacent to the common border may be supported.
OBJECTIVES AND SCOPE OF CONTROL
On 11 December 2007, the European Commission (EC) approved the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007–2013 (OPPS ČR–POL).
258,187,467 € were earmarked for the implementation of the Operational Programme.
The total amount included a contribution from European Funds (ERDF) totalling 219,459,344 € (85 %) which must be used by the end of 2015.
The financial data on the drawdown of EU funds from the Operational Programme for Cross-Border Cooperation Czech Republic – Republic of Poland 2007–2013 at the end of 2013 are presented in the following table:
The Agreement on Cooperation between Supreme Audit Institutions of the Czech Republic (SAIs) and the Republic of Poland (SAIs) was concluded by the presidents of both authorities on 27 February 2013 after previous mutual negotiations at the meeting of the Heads of Supreme Audit Institutions of the Visegrad Four, Austria and Slovenia, which took place in September 2012 in Lovasberény, Hungary.
The aim of the cooperation was to verify whether the implementation of the Operational Programme proceeds in accordance with legal regulations and with binding programming documents or agreements, whether the management and control system works reliably, whether the objectives of the Operational Programme are achieved and whether the selected projects are implemented in accordance with the established conditions of the provided support.
This cooperation took the form of a parallel audit, in which the Audit Authorities, based on their legal mandate, independently examined topics whose content had been jointly discussed beforehand.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The audit also included elements of a performance audit.
The selected topics were examined on a sample of projects at project partners on both sides of the border.
The results of the audits were audit conclusions published in the usual manner by both Authorities.
Based on the two audit conclusions mentioned in the annex, this joint report was created.
The selected joint audit sample included six projects (Annex 1) which fall within the support area 1.1 – Strengthening transport accessibility and support area 2.2 – Supporting tourism development.
Before the start of the audit, the drawing of funds from these two support areas represented more than 60% of the funds reimbursed to beneficiaries by the end of 2012 from the ERDF for all implemented projects within the whole OPPS CR–POL.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In addition to the audit of a joint sample of six projects, the SAO audited another 13 projects and NIK audited another two (all from the support area 1.1 and 2.2).
The funds paid to beneficiaries from the ERDF, which were audited by the SAO and NIK, totalled 25,362,174 € (SAO) and 8,450,784 € (NIK).
The audited amount of funds represented 41% of all funds paid to beneficiaries from the support areas 1.1 and 2.2.
MAIN CONCLUSIONS
Both audits carried out independently by the SAO on the Czech side and NIK on the Polish side showed that the level of identified irregularities did not exceed the level of significance.
Implementation of the OPPS CR–POL, including the functioning of the management and control system and the implementation of selected projects, was successful.
The audited persons, who were part of the management and control system, took steps to reduce the risk of misuse of funds and non-use of the provided funds from the ERDF.
Proof of the effectiveness of the implemented projects under the operational programme is the high level of achievement of the stated objectives, which was supported by the results of the survey of the social evaluation of the implementation of the projects.
SUMMARY OF THE RESULTS OF THE CONTROL
A. Evaluation of the management and control system
The coordinated approach made it possible to carry out the control of the management and control system of the OPPS CR–POL implemented by the following institutions in both countries: the managing authority (Ministry for Regional Development of the CR), the national coordinator (Ministry for Regional Development of the POL) and the controllers (Centre for Regional Development and Voivodship).
At the same time, the coordinated audit made it possible to evaluate the tasks and functions carried out by the joint technical secretariat and the monitoring committee.
During the evaluation of the management system, the audit focused on activities carried out in connection with the setting and performance of the functions of the managing authority and the national coordinator, especially in connection with the setting of programme objectives and monitoring of the achieved progress, evaluation activities and annual reports, the assessment, evaluation, selection and approval of project applications, drawing of programme funds and fulfilling the obligation to publish correct, complete and up-to-date information on grants, the control activities of the managing authority, setting of performance of activities and outcomes from audits of the delegated audit body and with the system of reporting and monitoring of irregularities and its compliance, at the same time, the coordination of tasks and cooperation between institutions involved in the OPPS CR–POL was examined.
Furthermore, the activities of the competent authorities in the area of risk analysis and drawing up of control plans at the place of implementation of projects were examined and evaluations of project applications carried out by independent evaluators were assessed.
The audit system included a review of administrative and on-the-spot audits, which allowed approval of e.g. deliveries of goods and co-financed services, eligibility of expenses submitted for reimbursement, and compliance of the disbursement of these funds and related activities (or parts thereof) with national and EU legislation.
Significant deficiencies were found by the SAO audit in the area of publishing correct, complete and up-to-date information on the provided subsidies.
In the area of evaluation and approval of projects, a risk of non-transparency of this process was found.
Other deficiencies were found in the area of setting of monitoring indicators and in the setting of the irregularity reporting system.
Based on these findings, the Supreme Audit Office assessed the control and management system implemented by the managing authority as partially effective.
Irregularities found by the NIK, which related to the functioning of the control and control system in Poland in relation to violations of applicable laws and rules, were not evaluated as significant.
These irregularities had an impact only on individual projects and did not have a significant impact on the implementation of the whole OPPS CR–POL.
Despite the identified irregularities, the NIK evaluated the functioning of the system positively.
B. Evaluation of project results
The audit did not show a discrepancy between indicators and objectives set for individual projects and those defined by the operational programme.
In all audited projects, the expected results were achieved, the funded activities were implemented and the indicators (measurable parameters) set in the project applications of the operational programme were met.
The audit conclusions are in line with the results of the survey of the social evaluation of project implementation described below.
An important aspect that the SAO and the SAI paid attention to in their conclusions is the limited possibility of objective evaluation of the fulfilment of the expected results of individual projects with regard to the objectives of individual projects and at the same time to the objectives of the entire Operational Programme.
On the initiative of the Supreme Audit Chamber, a public opinion survey was carried out in the summer of 2013 at the place of implementation of projects selected in the joint audit sample.
The survey and its evaluation were carried out by the School of Higher Vocational Training in Nysa (Poland).
In agreement with the Supreme Audit Office, the survey was carried out on both sides of the border between 2,820 respondents, both among the public in Poland in relation to the part of the project implemented by Polish project partners and among the Czech public in relation to the part of the project implemented in the Czech Republic.
The survey mainly concerned the suitability, quality, exchange of information, use of funds and social function of projects including the implemented investments.
The number of respondents for individual projects is given in Annex 2.
The assessment of the survey responses indicated that the overall social rating of the projects was good and reached 4.8 points (on a scale with a maximum of 8 points), which means that the results of the projects were positively perceived by the majority of respondents both in terms of the objectives and other parameters mentioned in the project applications.
According to respondents, the projects examined fulfilled the criteria expressed in the operational programme.
Respondents also noted the positive impact of the investments on improving the quality of transport and tourism infrastructure, transport accessibility, tourist attractiveness, business conditions, quality of life of citizens and resumption of contacts between residents of the border region.
On the other hand, the overall assessment of the responses indicated a certain difference between the actual level of fulfilment of the objectives/parameters (the actual level of impact) and the level expected by respondents.
This difference relates to the evaluation of the results of individual projects, where in some cases there is a significant difference.
The survey results were in line with the opinions of participants of panel discussions (target groups) carried out as part of the NIK audit on the Polish side, who pointed out the need for the implementation of the projects and confirmed their positive acceptance by the public and the fulfilment of the expected results.
C. Evaluation of legality of audited projects
The beneficiaries were checked for compliance of the material and time implementation of the project with the timetable and content of the project application, fulfilment of the stated objectives and purpose of the project, fulfilment of binding monitoring indicators, management of changes in the projects, treatment of potential project revenues, fulfilment of the obligation to keep separate accounting records of project transactions, compliance with the prohibition of financing projects from other EU sources, implementation of the project partnership and compliance with its conditions, fulfilment of the obligatory publicity concerning, in particular, the dissemination of awareness of the sources of financing projects from the EU, compliance with the conditions of eligibility of project expenses, selection of contractors and verification of the delivered fulfilment on the basis of the awarded public contracts.
The total financial value of the deficiencies classified by the SAO and NIK as ineligible expenditure from EU funds was found to be 85,094 €.
However, this amount did not exceed the stated level of materiality related to the total audited volume of funds for the sample of projects.
Despite the detected irregularities, the SAO and the SAI evaluated the implementation of selected projects positively by the beneficiaries.
In most cases, the commitments resulting from the conditions of support were duly fulfilled by the beneficiaries.
The funds intended for the implementation of audited projects were spent in accordance with EU regulations and national law.
Nevertheless, seven out of eight beneficiaries audited by the SAI were found to have irregularities in the implementation of material and financial commitments.
These were individual cases of a non-systemic nature and related to particularly inconsistent preparation of project documentation and breaches of public procurement rules, which did not have an impact on the selection of the contractor or the delivery of contractually agreed works.
In only one of all projects audited by NIK, the irregularity found, which related to the non-fulfilment of reporting obligations, can be classified as ineligible expenditure, amounting to 43 003 PLN (of which 8 231 € financed by the ERDF).
On the other side of the border, the SAO identified ineligible expenditure amounting to 2 098 053 CZK (of which 76 863 € financed by the ERDF) in three of the 19 projects audited.
Irregularities included the non-delivery of paid materials and works or expenditure for the modernisation of a property owned by a person who had no connection to the project either as a beneficiary of the aid or as a project partner.
RECOMMENDATIONS
The joint recommendation of the SAO and NIK concerns the improvement of the monitoring system, in particular with regard to the consistent establishment of proper monitoring indicators for all specific objectives of the priority axis of the operational programme.
In general, it is also necessary to strive for the establishment of such indicators at the programme and project level, which will allow more reliable verification of the fulfilment of the determined impacts.
Comments and recommendations resulting from inspections carried out on both sides of the border are included in separate audit conclusions of the SAO and the SAI on CD.
COMMUNICATIONS
The report was prepared in Czech, English and Polish languages and signed by the presidents of the supreme audit institutions of the Czech Republic and the Republic of Poland.
Public works procurement and corruption prevention
Joint report on parallel audits carried out by the Czech Supreme Audit Office (SAO) and the German Federal Court of Auditors (BRH)
Introduction and overview
Public procurement of goods and services by contracting authorities is very important for both the state and the private sector.
The role of the state is to use public resources in the most efficient and economical way and to ensure fair and regulated free competition.
In the European Union (EU), public procurement is a key factor for the success of the single economic area.
The EU has set limits in its regulations above which the contracting authority must comply with certain requirements designed to guarantee a wide and unlimited competitive environment throughout the EU.
EU Member States must therefore transpose European procurement law into their national legislation and put it into practice.
Both the Czech Republic and Germany have already done so.
For public contracts below the EU thresholds, the regulation of their awarding remains the responsibility of individual countries.
In addition, public procurement is prone to corruption with a consequent high potential for damage.
Therefore, contracting authorities are obliged to take reasonable measures in their procurement procedures to prevent corruption and unfair favouritism (prevention of corruption).
In January 2011, the Supreme Audit Office of the Czech Republic (Czech SAI – SAO) and the Federal Court of Auditors of Germany (German SAI – BRH) agreed to perform parallel audits in the area of procurement procedures and prevention of corruption in land and transport constructions.
The cooperation was also focused on the area of European procurement law as transposed into national legislation.
The audits also included awarding contracts below the European thresholds in order to sanction the area of prevention of corruption.
The audit findings of the two supreme audit institutions are summarised in a joint report, which is among others addressed to international organizations such as the International Organization of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI).
Audit areas and audited institutions
The evaluation of the BRH is based on general audits of the aforementioned areas, which were carried out at the German building administrations responsible for federal transport and land constructions between 2011 and 2012.
For example, motorway constructions and large-scale construction projects were evaluated.
The BRH also included findings from past audits in the evaluation.
The evaluation of the SAO is based on audits carried out during the years 2008 to 2012, which focused on the construction of roads, railway lines, water projects, large buildings (hospitals, cultural facilities, universities) and some other constructions in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, federal public procurement is mostly carried out by the regional building administrations.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement in the area of transport infrastructure is the responsibility of state institutions for road and railway construction.
In the area of building construction, public procurement is carried out by entities that receive budgetary funds.
In both countries, the services of external experts are also used in the individual stages of the procurement procedure.
The evaluation must take into account differences in public procurement legislation and the specificities of the construction market in both countries.
Structure of the Public Procurement Act in both countries
In Germany, the course of European procurement procedures is governed by laws and sub-statutory standards.
Procurement procedures limited to Germany are governed by budgetary law and relevant administrative acts .
Internal administrative rules contain numerous additional provisions for both European and national procurement procedures.
Further details regarding the requirements of European procurement law and the prevention of corruption are enshrined in the so-called procurement manuals of the federal government, in which the requirements are elaborated or formulated in greater detail with regard to legal precedents in the field of procurement law.
In the Czech Republic, public procurement is governed by the Public Procurement Act and other legislation that relates to contracts with a value above and below European thresholds.
Procurement law underwent substantial changes in 2012.
The new rules reinforced the transparency of procurement procedures, opened up space for more competition and limited opportunities for corruption in the course of procurement procedures.
Basic aspects of procurement law in both countries
As regards European procurement procedures, open and restricted procedures in particular must be applied in both countries.
In Germany, contracts below the European threshold are awarded in accordance with national rules (public call for competition, restricted call for competition, negotiated procedures), however, these are practically identical to the above-mentioned conditions for European procurement procedures.
Public procurement in Germany is characterised by the principle of preference for open procedures (where candidates are invited on a European scale) and public calls for participation (only in the case of national procedures).
In open procedures and in calls for competition, any candidate can submit a bid.
Another important feature of German procurement law is the support of small and medium-sized firms.
This is achieved mainly by dividing the total public contract into business-specific parts and by dividing it into sub-contracts.
By awarding contracts divided into these parts, the chances of participating in the procedure are given to the largest possible number of candidates.
The two characteristics of German procurement law are time-tested tools for obtaining good value for money.
There are more than 330,000 construction companies with 1.9 million employees registered in Germany.
In the Czech Republic, the conditions of European procedures must be applied also in cases of public procurement with a value below the EU limit.
An exception is the simplified below-threshold procedure, where the contracting authority may use less demanding procedures.
In accordance with European law, Czech procurement law includes the conditions under which the contract must be awarded in order to ensure competition and achieve a favourable price.
Current Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to choose between an open procedure and a restricted procedure.
There are 85,000 construction companies registered in the Czech Republic, of which about 1,900 have 20 or more employees.
Total volume of contracting authorities' public works contracts and proportion of individual procedures in both countries
In 2011, the total volume of construction works in Germany amounted to EUR 7 675 billion.
CZK 1,138 billion (15 %) of which was accounted for by the share of public contracts which, in addition to those awarded by the federal government, included contracts awarded by individual Länder and municipalities.
The total volume of projects of federal road and land constructions (without the construction of railways and water constructions) amounted to CZK 212.5 billion.
In 2011, the composition of federal works and transport contracts was as follows: 80,7 % open procedures and public calls; 12,7 % restricted management and calls;
6.6% negotiated procedures and direct award of contracts; 33.2% is the share of European procurement procedures.
The volume of public construction contracts in the Czech Republic reached 118.6 billion CZK in 2011, which was about 25% of the total volume of all construction works.
In 2009, the value reached CZK 194.0 billion, which was 37% of the total.
Structure of tender types in 2011: 65,1 % open procedures; 14,3 % restricted procedures; 9.5% negotiated procedures; 8.4% simplified procedures for low-threshold contracts; 2.7% other proceedings.
The figures include all public supply, service and works contracts awarded by contracting authorities in 2011.
The figures show that: parallel controls in both countries were focused on a substantial part of public procurement procedures; prevention of corruption, which must be kept in mind during procurement procedures, has a significant financial impact; open significance. procurement procedures are of key importance.
Summary of main results
The SAO and BRH carried out parallel controls on the procurement of European works contracts with an emphasis on prevention of corruption in their respective countries.
The audits were focused on comparing the legislation in both countries and the EU and on the implementation of public contracts in the field of land and transport construction.
The following are the key findings:
The EU Public Procurement Directive has been transposed into the national laws of both countries (points 1 and 3.1).
Prevention of corruption is not only the aim of the EU Directive, but also the national procurement rules of both countries, which differ partly in terms of focus and details.
In both countries, the basic requirement to be met by the procurement documents is the inclusion of all information needed for the preparation of tenders.
The specifications must be neutral and must not contain any requirements restricting competition.
The procurement documents in Germany generally meet these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the problem is an insufficient definition of the subject matter of the contract.
In Germany, the division of contracts into parts where possible is required in order to promote a competitive environment.
According to the BRH's findings, this requirement is respected in Germany.
The SAO's audits showed that in the Czech Republic very large public works in the field of road construction are awarded without being divided into parts or only into very significant parts in volume. This method can distort competition and lead to restrictions on only a few large companies.
These limitations on market potential can result in significant cost increases and risk of unserious price negotiations and inadmissible agreements.
The legislation in both countries allows contracting authorities, under certain conditions, to award additional works retrospectively if the need arises after the conclusion of the contract (point 4.1).
In both countries, the requirements for correctly determining the type of procurement procedure are met.
In the Czech Republic, under the applicable rules, contracting authorities are free to determine one of the open procurement procedures, namely the open procedure or the restricted procedure.
The German rules in procurement and budget law enshrine the preference for open procedures for both European tenders and national tenders, known as public calls for competition.
The open procurement procedures in both countries allow a larger range of candidates to submit a tender and also reduce the risk of corruption as they make it more difficult to negotiate prices illegally between candidates.
At the same time, the best value for money results are achieved.
In the case of large contracts where the estimated total price reaches or exceeds the EU thresholds, the audited contracting entities have always issued a European tender.
The correct estimation of the estimated value of the contract plays a very important role in determining the type of tender.
In Germany, databases are used for objective pricing of public works, in which values are based on completed construction projects.
This is a sufficiently objective tool.
In the Czech Republic, databases are used only in the field of road construction, but they are not yet an effective tool (point 4.2).
When awarding a public works contract under European tendering procedures, it is necessary to publish prior information and a contract notice in order to strengthen competition by allowing candidates to prepare in time for the submission of tenders.
As a rule, the construction audited impad with this requirement.
In isolated cases, errors in the information released, incorrect application of provisions and/or a lack of controls may have impaired competition (item 4.3).
When selecting candidates to participate in a restricted procurement procedure, there is a greater risk of manipulation.
Under EU law, the number of candidates invited must be high enough to guarantee genuine competition.
Selection criteria must be objective, non-discriminatory and published well in advance.
Beyond the EU requirements, contracting entities in both countries should rotate as often as possible candidates invited to participate in the procedure when selecting suppliers.
In Germany, there are partly very detailed requirements to prevent risks of manipulation.
In some cases, the audited contracting entities did not follow these requirements.
In the Czech Republic, selection decisions were not always credible (section 4.4).
In both countries, contracting entities must ensure during the procurement procedure that all candidates who wish to submit a tender receive the same information.
Any agreements between these candidates and between them and third parties involved in the preparation of the specifications must be prevented as far as possible in order to avoid restricting competition in the form of illegal agreements.
In Germany, the relevant measures are more thorough than in the Czech Republic.
However, the audited German contracting entities did not comply with this requirement in several cases (point 4.5).
In both countries, the principle of cross-checking and transparency must be applied when opening envelopes.
The requirements on the marking, documentation, protection and confidentiality of tenders are intended to limit the scope for manipulation and in Germany they are particularly detailed.
Checks in both countries found only a few cases of irregularities (point 4.6).
The evaluation and evaluation of tenders is regulated procedurally in both countries.
The relevant German measures are more comprehensive, but they were not always respected by the audited contracting entities.
For example, contracting authorities passed their tenders to third parties for consultation without prior assessment, without properly documenting any suspicious circumstances.
Thus, tenders were insufficiently protected against subsequent manipulation (point 4.7).
For reasons of legal protection, bidders must be informed about the intended award of the contract before the contract is finalised.
This legal protection award is designed to prevent corruptive procedures before the contract is finalised.
The law of the Czech Republic provides for judicial review of the contracts for national contractors.
Only in few cases, the due review of the enterprises was not ensured (item 4.8).
The tender documentation was not always complete or fully credible for the audited contracting authorities in both countries.
In the case of several procurement procedures, the transparency requirements applied at European level were not always adequately respected in the tender minutes.
In addition, some deficiencies identified reduced the preventive anti-corruption function of the documentation.
Contracting entities in both countries mostly fulfilled their reporting obligations (point 4.9).
Summary of the main principles of European procurement law and prevention of corruption
European procurement law
In order to ensure free competition in the EU, the European Parliament and the Council of the European Union adopted, inter alia, a Directive on the coordination of procedures for the award of public works contracts, public supply contracts and public service contracts to ensure the free movement of goods and services and a Directive on the coordination of laws, regulations and administrative provisions relating to the application of review procedures to the award of public supply contracts and public works contracts (the Remedies Directive), both of which were to be transposed into national law.
The EU's general procurement principles include in particular: a requirement for a competitive environment; a requirement for equal treatment or non-discrimination; a requirement for transparency.
EU procurement principles include In addition, the Directive on the coordination of public procurement procedures includes provisions concerning construction projects with a total estimated value of €5 million or more.
These provisions affect: the content of the procurement documents; the type of procurement procedure used; the publication of certain information before, during and after the conclusion of the procedure; compliance with the deadline for the receipt of tenders; the qualification requirements that tenderers may be required to meet; the criteria for selecting the most suitable tender.
The legal protection of tenderers is governed by the Remedies Directive.
In case of potential breaches of procurement law, tenderers may have the contracting authority's decision reviewed according to the review procedures.
Both countries have transposed EU requirements into national legislation.
Preventing corruption
The importance of preventing corruption
There is no legal definition of what corruption actually means in either country.
In general, corruption is understood as an abuse of a personal (confidential) position for one's own benefit.
As a rule, this means that such abuse is instigated by those who gain from it.
Corruption can be an obstacle to competition and can cause serious economic losses.
Overall corruption-related damage is estimated at 5% of world economic output.
For the EU area, these costs are estimated at 1% of the EU's economic output.
When a contracting authority is involved in a contract, conventions restricting competition or allowing dishonest behaviour can weaken confidence in the integrity, capacity and overall work of the public administration.
Therefore, preventing corruption is an essential task for contracting authorities.
Practices and procedures need to be checked in particular in areas particularly susceptible to corruption so that adequate measures can be taken to prevent it.
Taking preventive measures and carrying out effective checks can substantially reduce the susceptibility of the decision-making process to corruption.
As a rule, the award of works contracts is considered to be a particularly susceptible area to corruption.
Measures to prevent corruption
In Germany, corruption is prosecutable under criminal and labour law, which particularly concerns active and passive bribery, the receipt and offering of significant benefits.
The German federal government has issued a manual of recommendations concerning the prevention of corruption in the federal administration, according to which the following principles are applied throughout the procurement process, outside risk analysis: the preference for open procurement; the principle of multiple control and transparent decision-making; the separation of the planning, procurement and accounting phases; the accurate and complete documentation of the procurement procedure.
In addition, other measures having a preventive effect against corruption have become part of the procurement law.
In 2011, the competent federal ministry of information technology introduced a system for monitoring the award of federal road construction contracts.
Using this system, data such as estimated costs, total value of the contract and involvement of private design offices can be fully recorded and analysed to identify potential irregularities.
In the Czech Republic, corruption is prosecuted under criminal law, including the employment or civil law dimension.
The legal requirement of transparency, equal treatment and the prohibition of any discrimination throughout the procurement process (preparation and implementation) is the most important part of preventing corruption.
Anti-corruption measures in this area were set by the government in the "Government's Anti-Corruption Strategy 2011-2012", which was updated for 2013 and 2014.
Part of these measures were incorporated into the Public Procurement Act and subsequent implementing regulations.
The new legislation contains the following basic provisions: lowering the thresholds for small-scale contracts; the obligation of prior notification by the contracting authority including the justification for the contract; the prohibition on limiting the number of tenderers in restricted procedures and the abolition of the general possibility to limit the number of tenderers by drawing lots; the obligation to publish contracts including annexes; the obligation to publish the actual and lists of subcontractors.
Results of checks relating to subsequent stages of the procurement procedure
Tender documents
Tender documents must contain all the information needed to enable tenderers to prepare their tenders in an appropriate form.
In particular, the documents must make it possible to prepare the tender price.
To make this possible, tenderers must not be exposed to risks that they could not have anticipated.
Tender documents contain mainly specifications, as well as requirements to prove the qualifications of tenderers and criteria for selecting the most advantageous tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
In particular, European law contains requirements relating to technical specifications.
The specifications must limit competition as little as possible and all candidates must have equal access to them.
The specifications must be neutral.
Requirements for specific products are permissible in the procurement documents on a very limited scale.
The basic principle of prevention of corruption is that the procurement documents do not contain requirements that could restrict competition.
Furthermore, the documentation should avoid ambiguities or inaccurate data that create room for speculation and manipulation in the evaluation of tenders.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have transposed EU legal provisions into their national legislation.
The basic principle is that the specifications should be given in general terms without reference to a specific product or brand.
The specifications must precisely define the supplies and services required and detail the number of units of work and supplies.
In normal cases, prices should be given by units of work or supplies (e.g. in CZK/m or in CZK/item).
It is permissible to define the required works in terms of their functionality only in exceptional cases.
In principle, the rule is that specifications must be drawn up completely and unambiguously.
They must contain precise quantities and must not include works and supplies whose necessity is not entirely obvious.
Payment in the form of lump sums or time worked must be avoided wherever possible.
These parts of the procurement documents must exclude speculation and manipulation, based on ambiguous specifications.
The specifications must allow each tenderer to prepare his tender with certainty and without excessive preparatory work.
The invitation to tender must be published only when the procurement documents are complete.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation for contracting authorities to award construction contracts in parts.
A contracting authority may divide a public contract into several separate lots if the nature of the subject of such a contract allows it.
In Germany, the application of the rule of dividing works into lots is required, either quantitatively (partial deliveries) or according to specific aspects (specialised parts of the contract).
This allows participation in the competition by small and medium-sized entities, increases the number of potential bidders and makes it more difficult to fix prices.
This type of award is usually considered the most suitable in terms of providing value for money.
In the Czech Republic, the tender dossier must contain the evaluation criteria in both national and European procurement procedures.
This should inform the tenderer comprehensively and exhaustively about the criteria by which his tender will be evaluated.
In Germany, the requirement to include the evaluation criteria in the notice applies only to European calls for tender.
In national tendering procedures, it is necessary to include evaluation criteria only in the contract record, which is an internal administrative document.
If the tender documents are prepared by authorised independent experts, German regulations require any information that would help to identify specific interested firms, architects or designers to be excluded.
The reason is to prevent individual construction firms from obtaining any additional information or other undue competitive advantages through contacts with these independent experts without the knowledge of the contracting authority.
This is also the reason why independent experts are not allowed to publish documents relating to tenders and to make them accessible for verification.
The contracting authority is the only point of contact for construction firms.
The Czech Republic currently does not have a similar provision.
In the case of large construction projects, there is almost always a need for additional works or modification of existing works after the conclusion of the original contract.
The legislation of both countries allows for the award of a contract for additional works up to a certain limit to the same contractor without the need to publish and carry out a new procurement procedure.
The volume of additional works under both European and German legislation may not exceed 50% of the original value of the contract.
In addition, additional works must be necessary and have the character of a special part of the main contract.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% is applied for the award of additional works.
This may entail the risk of a greater time lag in the execution of the contract and the possible inclusion of a new entity in an already running project.
If the contract for additional works is awarded to the contractor of the original contract, German law contains a provision that prevents contractors from arbitrarily increasing the price of modified works or additional works.
Calculations must be made taking into account the prices agreed in the original contract.
In addition to additional works, there may also be less work during construction, i.e. works that were not needed.
In the Czech Republic, less work and additional works cannot be compensated for each other.
Main control findings
BHR
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tender
In rare cases, specifications referred to national technical requirements or standards, but these were replaced by EU standards in the meantime.
In some cases, specifications included data requesting a particular brand name, e.g. for the resistance system of public signage, to prevent water infiltration or for anti-noise cladding, although this was not necessary.
In some cases, the specifications were so detailed that only one product met the requirements.
Quantity specifications broken down into lots
In not a single case did the BRH record a check of procurement procedures of non-compliance with the general requirements for a call for tender for supplies and services under the quantity specifications broken down into lots.
Completeness of specifications
In most of the cases audited, tender documents were entirely drawn up before the call for tender was announced.
In various cases, however, the documents had to be modified during the procurement procedure when changes in lots or in the timetable had to be taken into account.
In rare cases, tender documents contained works and supplies for which the need was uncertain.
One tender document for demolition works contained almost exclusively such lots.
Division of the contract and award criteria
In many cases, the required justification for not dividing the supplies into lots was not demonstrated.
The BRH did not find any shortcomings in the criteria for evaluation of tenders before the award of the contract.
Inclusion of the names of authorised independent experts
Many documents mentioned interested authorised experts.
The names were given on the opening pages of the documents or on the plans or attached opinions.
In one case, the contracting authority even invited tenderers to contact an explicitly appointed architect with their questions on the procurement procedure.
In the case of building projects, these cases were found mainly in the tender documents, where the elaboration of the specifications was entrusted to self-employed professionals.
The contracting authorities stated that they did not have sufficient staff capacity for detailed examination of the tender documents.
SAO
Technical specifications and product-neutral call for tender
In the case of a public contract for the construction of a house with 12 flats, the reference was directly to specific manufacturers and brands of products.
The contracting authority did not allow the use of other similar types of products nor did it justify why it required the delivery of specific products.
In the case of the construction of facilities of a university, the tender documents included references to specific commercial firms and specific product names.
Overview of specifications and partial specifications
For the construction of certain industrial zones, the contracting authority did not clearly define in the tender documentation the quantity and type of works, supplies or services required in the statement of acreage and defined for each building only the enclosed space in cubic meters as the unit of measurement.
Completeness of specifications
During the procurement procedure for the construction of a road ring, the tender documentation for the restricted procedure was changed four times between August and September 2009.
This was due to changes in the project documentation caused by the extension of the scope of the contract and the change of technical conditions.
For the construction of social buildings, the tender documentation had defects that forced the creation of additional works (lack of precise focus, lack of sufficient technical survey, lack of drawings).
In the tender documentation for the construction of a large library, the interior elements were mentioned only in descriptive terms, without specifying quality requirements for products.
The supply of the interior equipment of the building was ensured by subcontractors who purchased some above-standard and very expensive equipment.
Parts and criteria for the award of the contract
In the Czech Republic, there is no obligation to award the contract in parts.
The analysis of three large road constructions during the construction of a road ring showed that it was possible to achieve savings of up to CZK 790 million from the achieved public contract price of CZK 15,508 million by awarding them in the open procedure in parts (separately after selected objects).
For example, when awarding six building objects as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 62.5 million could be achieved, i.e.
25% of the contracted price and when awarding 20 bridges as a separate part of the public contract, savings of CZK 287.8 million, i.e. also 25%, could be achieved.
In the tender documentation, the contracting authority defined qualification criteria in the construction of roads, which limited the participation of small suppliers in the tender procedure.
In the tender conditions of the open tender for a supplier for one construction of a motorway, the contracting authority required, among other things, the practice of the supplier consisting in the realization of at least three road constructions with a financial volume of each more than CZK 700 million, completed or put into operation during the last five years.
There are only nine companies in the Czech Republic that meet the condition of completion of constructions above CZK 700 million in the last five years.
The public contract for the supply of another construction of a motorway in the open procedure included six separate constructions, for which the contracting authority required a minimum annual volume of construction works in the last three years of CZK 12 billion.
Two thirds of financial volume of road construction works (i.e. € 2.5 billion out of € 3.7 billion) in the period of 2008–2009 were realized by 5 suppliers or individually in form of consortium.
For 46 contracts for a total of CZK 13.2 billion, there were only two or even one bidder.
The SAO pointed out that the limitation of market potential can lead to a large increase in costs.
Area of additional works
In two cases, the contracting authority did not conclude a written contract for the supply of additional works and did not award any of the procedures stipulated in the Act.
The value of the non-approved additional works in one case amounted to CZK 1,591.4 million excluding VAT (increase by 21%) and in the other case to CZK 4,135.8 million excluding VAT (increase by 91%).
The reason for the additional works was mainly ambiguous definition of the amount and type of work required.
In the case of the construction of university facilities, the contracting authority did not include in the tender documentation some works and supplies whose necessity for the completion and approval of the construction was already known at the time of the contract award.
For these works and supplies, the contracting authority concluded amendments to the contract with the contractor of the construction with an increase in the price, in a negotiated procedure without publication, even though the volume of additional works exceeded the national limit of 20% of the value of the original contract.
In connection with the construction of the Palace of Justice, the contracting authority reserved the right to award additional works without a tender procedure.
During the construction, the contracting authority increased the scope of the contract by CZK 50.5 million.
Unit prices of additional works set without a tender were significantly higher than comparable works carried out by the same contractor.
Other findings
On a selected sample of buildings, the SAO found that the average construction costs for the construction of one kilometre of a four-lane motorway in the Czech Republic were CZK 355.0 million.
According to long-term data obtained from the German Ministry of Construction, the average costs in Germany for a kilometre section of a four-lane motorway are CZK 260.0 million.
This comparison indicates that the acquisition of a motorway in the Czech Republic is more expensive than in Germany.
Summary
The legal provisions relating to the tender documentation are almost identical in both countries and comply with the requirements of European law.
The aim is to ensure full and equal competition and to prevent corruption.
The specifications must be sufficiently precise to allow candidates to calculate their costs and prepare their bids.
Moreover, this requirement limits the scope for speculation and the contracting authority is protected against the need to award additional works.
In Germany, the basic legal provisions on procurement documents are supplemented by other rules designed to further reduce the possibilities for limiting competition and for carrying out manipulations.
Although there are no detailed requirements of this kind in the Czech Republic, greater transparency is achieved by publishing the award criteria.
Specification requirements are mostly met in both countries.
However, in some cases, shortcomings have been identified which could lead to a reduction of competition.
The current rule in Germany requiring large construction projects to be divided into parts seems to be suitable for strengthening the competitive environment and achieving cost-effective results.
The SAO found that in the Czech Republic, awarding contracts for large-scale projects without awarding them in parts or dividing them into large-scale projects may limit the pool of candidates to a small number of large suppliers.
As a result of the strict requirement for bidder references applied in the Czech Republic for the selection of bidders for road construction works, public contracts are awarded to a small number of bidders and small and medium-sized companies have no chance to compete.
The limited number of bidders also increases the risk of fixing prices and other illegal agreements.
As regards awarding additional contracts for extra works or modified works and their reimbursement, regulatory approaches are different in both countries.
If the value of the additional works under European and German law does not exceed 50% of the value of the original contract, the extra works may be awarded without a procurement procedure and its notification.
In the Czech Republic, a stricter limit of 20% applies.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
This limit is often exceeded by the contracting authorities.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If the limit of 20% is exceeded, the extra works must be awarded on the basis of a new procurement procedure.
By dividing works into lots, restricting competition and consequent adverse consequences can be avoided.
Determining the type of procurement procedure with regard to EU limits
By determining the type of procurement procedure, the contracting entity defines the range of potential suppliers that will be admitted to the competition.
The contracting entity can leave the range of potential suppliers unlimited, or it can limit the range according to the given criteria.
In the case of large project works whose value reaches or exceeds the EU limits, a European procurement procedure must be conducted.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
In choosing the type of procedure, the contracting entity must comply in particular with the requirements of European law concerning the competitive environment and transparency.
If the estimated total cost of the contract for the requested works reaches or exceeds the current EU limit of €5.0 million ($125 million), an open or restricted procurement procedure at European level is always required.
In exceptional cases, a negotiated procedure or a competitive dialogue is permissible.
If the construction project reaches the EU threshold, the contracting authority may deviate from the European procurement procedure to a limited extent by issuing a national procurement procedure if the total value of the relevant part is below the threshold of €1.0 million ($25 million) and the total value of all parts does not exceed 20% of the value of the estimated total contract.
Dividing a construction project or intentionally estimating its cost too low with the intention of avoiding the fundamental obligation to issue a European procurement procedure is not permissible.
Choosing the type of procedure is very important for preventing corruption, as restricting competition substantially increases the risk of manipulation.
Therefore, a detailed justification of the chosen type of procedure, in particular in relation to the obligation to apply European procurement law, is an important part of the procurement procedure documentation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU legal requirements for procurement procedures and limits in their national legislation.
Different provisions
The Czech Republic has introduced the principle of equal validity of open and restricted procedures also for procurement procedures at national level.
Open and restricted procedures can always be chosen, while other types of procedures are allowed only if the statutory conditions are met.
Unlike the Czech legislation, the relevant German regulation of budget law and principles of prevention of corruption prefers open procedures or a public call for participation in the procedure.
Other types of procedures, including restricted procedures, are allowed only exceptionally, if the legislation allows it.
Such exceptions must be justified and documented.
The principle of preference for open procedures is intended to guarantee that restricting competition to the maximum extent possible and that the opportunity for manipulation (pre-selected specific candidates) is limited.
In Germany, this principle of public procurement is central to the prevention of corruption.
A correct estimation of the expected contract price is an important basis for deciding on the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
When estimating the costs of building projects, contracting authorities in Germany can rely on budgetary and cost databases of public administrations and other organisations.
These have been compiled on the basis of an analysis of completed building projects and include, among other things, standard cost values for building types, building units, building components and partial works.
On the basis of such standard cost values, it is possible to estimate the value of the contract of the planned project or works.
Only a few building administrations in Germany already have a similar database for road works.
However, it is expected that the database will be expanded at national level.
Contracting authorities in the Czech Republic do not use any central database or average prices of comparable objects for determining the expected costs of building contracts.
In many cases, the determination of costs is based on an estimation of the entity financing the project or based on the value of the resources available to that entity.
Measures for standardisation are still at an early stage.
Under the Anti-Corruption Programme 2011-2012, the relevant ministries are to develop a database of the usual prices of standardised commodities.
The use of the price system to determine the expected price of the contract is determined by a new regulation ordering to specify in detail the subject of the contract.
The price system should serve as a set of information on construction and assembly works, materials and products and other data to calculate the necessary costs and to establish unit prices.
The Ministry of Transport has issued normative prices for transport works for their control, which are regularly updated.
The database is intended to determine the price of the project (investment) plan and should play a comparative function in the next stages of preparation of the works.
BRH's main control findings
Selection of the procurement procedure
The audited contracting entities generally awarded contracts by open procedure or by means of a public call for participation, thus complying with the requirements of the budget and procurement legislation.
In 2011, the share of tenders in the total volume of federal construction projects in the field of building construction was 59.5%.
In the road building sector, this share was 94.8%.
BRH found that in some cases the use of non-public tendering was not sufficiently justified.
In the case of the blanket control, BRH found, based on information collected from more than 16,000 procedures, that the restricted procedure has a clear disadvantage compared to the public procedure and the public call, and the restricted procedures also increase the risk of manipulation and corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
According to the BRH study, the tender prices in the restricted procedure and the negotiated procedure were between 6% and 10% higher than in the public procedures.
Parallel studies by the Ministry of Construction and the Land Audit Court confirmed differences in value for money obtained in the public and restricted procedures.
The study by the Ministry of Construction even found that the prices in the restricted procedures were twice as high as those in the public procedures.
Compliance with EU limits
For projects whose total value of the contract was estimated at more than €5 million ($125 million), the audited contracting authorities generally prepared procurement plans indicating which works would be awarded in the European procurement procedure and which in the national procedure.
According to the procurement plans, the share of works awarded under European rules always exceeded 80% of the total works.
No case of circumvention of the European procedure requirement was recorded in the form of an excessively low cost estimate.
However, in some cases the minimum share for the European-wide procedure was not reached due to a calculation error.
During the execution of the works, some contracting authorities illegally changed the procurement allocation plan.
