They did not give any reasons for these changes and did not show that the minimum share of the European procedure was subsequently respected.
The share of European procedures according to the project accounts was usually lower than the share set in the tender plan.
If the expected values of the construction projects' contracts exceeded the European limits, the contracting authorities did not apply the European procedure to all individual sub-parts with a value exceeding €1 million ($25 million).
Instead, they awarded the respective contracts on the basis of the national procedure without giving any valid reasons for the need to deviate from the requirement.
SAO
Choice of the tender procedure type
In the case of several large construction projects (e.g. construction of a road section, equipment for a major sporting event and construction of apartments), the contracting authority divided one project into several parts, even though these sub-contracts were of the same or similar nature.
The division reduced the expected value of the contract and the contracts were awarded in a less demanding mode or even outside the statutory regime.
Methods of calculating the expected price
Price norms applicable to road construction projects were not yet an effective tool for determining the expected price.
They were based on the prices indicated in the completed procedures, which were, however, burdened by inefficiencies and excessive profit of the suppliers.
The SAO found that the actual prices for three parts of the road circuit were approximately 37 % lower than these price norms.
The Ministry of Transport is to update the norms in relation not only to the prices from the past, but also in relation to other comparable markets and price development.
For ground constructions, the estimated price was based on the project budget carried out by architects or on an investor's estimate.
The actual price differed significantly from the expected price.
The estimated price of the construction of a large library was twice as high as the contract price, but during the implementation it increased by CZK 54 million, i.e. by 27 %.
Similarly, in the case of the construction of a depository of this library, the contract price was 33 % lower than the expected price.
In other cases, the price of the successful applicant was higher than the anticipated price and increased even more during the implementation.
For the construction of a large library, it was CZK 400 million, i.e. by 22%. For the construction of a palace of justice, the actual price increased disproportionately (by 139%) during the implementation of the construction, compared to the increase in functional areas (only by 12%).
Summary
Both countries have implemented European provisions on the choice of the type of procurement procedure and on compliance with European limits in their national legislation, so that the respective national regulations of both countries are almost identical.
However, the principle of public procedures in Germany is stricter than the European regulation and the current national regulation in the Czech Republic.
Compared to a narrower procurement procedure, a public procedure or a public call for participation gives the possibility for more competitors to participate in a procurement procedure.
These types of procurement procedures generate a large number of bids, which guarantee the best value for money effect.
The prevention of corruption is significantly reinforced as the pool of candidates is not limited by decisions taken in advance by the contracting authority, which thus gives room for manipulation and fixing of prices between bidders.
Studies have confirmed the advantages of the German provision on the preference of open procedures or public calls for participation.
Czech legislation allows contracting authorities to award contracts in open or narrower procedures.
However, the latest legislation of the Public Procurement Act introduces a prohibition for contracting authorities to limit the number of bidders in narrower procedures.
The narrower procedure has now come very close to the open procedure.
In Germany, contracting authorities are largely fulfilling the requirement of preference for open procedures or public calls for competition, while in the Czech Republic some projects were divided in violation of the law, in order to circumvent the requirement to choose the appropriate type of procurement procedure.
However, in the case of some projects implemented in Germany, European limits have not been respected, resulting in a lack of a minimum share in pan-European procedures.
In the Czech Republic, a satisfactory system has not yet been put in place to facilitate the correct calculation of the expected costs of works and projects.
Where appropriate, contracting entities should ensure, through more extensive controls, that the requirements to choose the type of procurement procedure and the volume of contracts awarded in European procedures are fulfilled, or they should create such conditions as are necessary to enable the correct calculation of the expected costs.
Preliminary information and notice of initiation
In cases where contracts are awarded in the framework of the European procedure, the contracting entity must publish a tender report.
Preliminary information and contract notice are an important basis for European competition.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
By means of prior notices, the contracting authority must indicate the basic specifications of the intended projects.
Preliminary notices are required for projects where the estimated total cost is equal to or greater than the European limit and when the contracting authority wishes to shorten the deadline for submitting tenders in individual procurement procedures.
The contracting authority is obliged to publish a contract award notice with detailed information for all public contracts requiring the European procedure.
The information is binding on the contracting authority after publication.
Among other things, the notice must contain the specifications, the time limits for the procedure and for completion of the contract, the specific requirements and selection criteria and their weightings.
A common procurement dictionary must be used to describe the subject of the contract.
Preliminary information and contract notices are published in the EU in the Official Journal of the EU and on the profile of the contracting authority.
In order to avoid discrimination against foreign candidates, notices at national level should not contain any additional information and should not be published before the text of the notice is sent to the Publications Office of the EU.
Incomplete or inaccurate information in a pre-notification may distort competition or even facilitate manipulation.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully implemented the EU provisions on pre-information and contract award notices in their national legislation.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, pre-information and notices must be published even in the case of contracts for which a European procedure is not required.
The procurement procedure may not start until one month after the pre-notification has been sent.
The publication of the pre-information may be waived only in exceptional cases.
In Germany, pre-information is not required in cases where a European procedure is not required.
In the case of national procedures, the public call must be published on the federal government website and, where appropriate, in the press.
In the case of restricted procedures without competition from participants, the notice must be published when the estimated contract price is equal to or higher than €25,000 ($625,000).
The prior information notices must explain all the planned parts of the project.
This is an important requirement given the number of small contracts in Germany.
The splitting of projects into smaller parts is not as common in the Czech Republic and this measure is considered to be less important.
Main control findings
BRH
Preliminary notice
Contracting authorities published a prior information notice for projects which included works to be awarded in the European procedure.
In some open procedures, they shortened the time limit, although the number of days between the prior information notice being sent and the contract notice being sent was less than 52 days.
In other abridged procedures, the annual time limits applicable to the prior information had expired and were to be renewed.
In many cases, the advance notice did not indicate the anticipated division of the works into several parts.
Procurement notice
Some notices contained codes from the common dictionary that deviated from the content of the advance notice or did not specify precisely the works to be carried out.
In rare cases, the notice at national level included more information, e.g. concerning performance deadlines, or was published before the notice was published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Ex-ante transparency
One contracting authority hired an external company to publish the national notice.
The company published the notice on its platform, the use of which was subject to a fee.
However, the contracting authority did not check whether the company had to comply with the requirement to send further notices to a free internet government portal.
On a sample, it was found that only one third of the notices were actually published on the government portal.
SAO
Procurement notice
In the construction of the wastewater treatment plant, the contracting authority did not set criteria in the notice of the tender procedure for the construction contractor, according to which it would reduce the large number of bids for the evaluation procedure.
The contracting authority was not able to demonstrate the method of selection (reduction) of the bids for the evaluation.
The reduction of the number of bids was allowed by previous legislation.
In the construction of the road circuit, the contracting authority did not define in the notice of the tender procedure or in the qualification documentation the values by which it would assess the degree of fulfilment of the qualification criteria or the number of bidders to be invited.
The contracting authority evaluated the fulfilment of the qualification so that all six candidates in the tender procedure fulfilled it, but excluded one of them as having fulfilled it only in the last sixth place.
The procedure of the contracting authority was not transparent as it did not publish in advance information on what of the qualification data would be evaluated and how.
Publication of invitations
For building modifications in former military installations, the contracting authority did not publish a written call for tenders in violation of the law.
Conclusions and recommendations / Summary
The two-stage information process with prior information and publication of a notice of initiation is an important tool for meeting the requirements for transparency and strengthening the competitive environment applied in European procedures.
By extending the obligation to publish prior information in the Czech Republic also for national procurement procedures and by strengthening this obligation in Germany for restricted procedures, both countries have increased the transparency of procurement procedures and improved the competitive environment.
Selection of candidates in restricted procurement procedures
Compared to open procedures, restricted procurement procedures do not allow participation of all potential bidders who would be interested.
In restricted procedures, the contracting authority first selects potential suppliers who will be asked to submit a tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
EU provisions allow to limit the number of bidders in European restricted procedures.
However, the restricted procedure requires at least five bidders to be invited to submit a tender, and in the case of a negotiated procedure with publication, at least three potential bidders must be invited.
In any case, the number of bidders invited must be sufficiently broad to guarantee genuine competition.
The selection criteria must be objective and non-discriminatory and must be published in advance.
The selection of potential bidders who will be invited is very susceptible to manipulation, e.g. the preferences of some of them.
Reducing competition to a few bidders can facilitate their collusion.
This has a negative impact on the outcome of the procedure.
Therefore, the contracting authority must take measures to prevent manipulation and ensure that it has effective control in order to make the procedure transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The provisions for the selection of bidders are similar in both countries.
In addition to the requirements of the European directive, contracting authorities should rotate the tenderers they invite to submit a tender.
It is also not permissible to restrict competition from a regional point of view.
Contracting authorities must document and justify the selection of tenderers and the selection process.
Different provisions
In Germany, at least three tenderers are required to be invited in the case of a restricted procedure and several suitable tenderers in the case of a negotiated procedure.
Except in two cases, the new Czech regulations do not allow limiting the number of tenderers approached in the case of a restricted and negotiated procedure.
In Germany, the "four eyes principle" must be respected in the selection of tenderers.
While one worker proposes their list, another must substantially modify the proposal, e.g. by removing the proposed tenderer or appointing another.
This is to avoid a situation where an individual can definitively determine the range of tenderers who will take part in the procedure.
If such a provision is not in place, a far-reaching illegal agreement between the participants is allowed.
The list of tenderers who will be invited to submit a tender must be confidential and must not be divulged.
Authorised independent experts are also obliged to do so.
There is no similar measure in the Czech Republic, nor is there any measure for confidential treatment of the list of tenderers.
According to the German provisions, external companies may not designate candidates, but they may make proposals to the contracting authority.
There is no similar requirement in the Czech Republic.
Main control findings
BRH
Number of candidates
Except in a few cases, the requirement for reaching the minimum number of tenderers was met.
An analysis of almost 7,000 works contracts with a value below the national limit showed that in the case of restricted procedures, contracting authorities invited an average of 6.6 potential tenderers and in the case of direct negotiation an average of 4.4 tenderers.
Change of contractors and regional restrictions
In particular in the field of building construction, contracting authorities did not change the pool of tenderers approached, as they had good experience with those with whom they had previously cooperated.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The contracting authorities argued that in the case of a non-public procedure, they should guarantee that they would reach out to candidates whose suitability they had already ascertained beforehand.
Although there was no evidence for illegal regional restrictions, the range of candidates in the non-public procedure included a greater proportion of regional tenderers than in the case of public procedures.
The contracting authorities explained this by the low level of the contract compared to the public procedure and the high cost of travel for potential tenderers.
Cross-checking principle and proposals of self-employed professionals
The contracting authorities mostly ignored the obligation to change the list of candidates proposed by self-employed professionals.
They mainly argued the lack of staff, time pressure or inadequate knowledge of a suitable tenderer.
In many cases, the records did not show who had proposed the candidate who had been asked to participate in the procedure.
A check of some samples showed that the list of potential tenderers had been drawn up by self-employed professionals and had been accepted by the contracting authority without changes.
The contracting authorities were thus insufficiently protected from potential manipulation.
Confidentiality of the treatment of lists of potential tenderers
One contracting authority kept a list of candidates for invitation to tender in a database to which all employees had access at any time.
This allowed for manipulation or unauthorised disclosure of the names of prospective or admitted tenderers, who could then agree on the price.
Documentation
The documentation gave the reasons for the selection of tenderers in only a few cases.
SAO
Criteria for the selection of tenderers
During the construction of the ring road, the candidates did not have information about what information about their qualifications would be evaluated and how.
For the action related to the development and modernisation of waterways and ports, the tender notice for participation in the restricted procedure did not set out criteria for limiting the number of tenderers.
All eight tenderers met the qualification requirements, but this exceeded the number to which the contracting authority had limited participation in the tender procedure.
The Commission then carried out the selection of candidates by random selection – by drawing lots, which was legal at the time.
This procedure should have been mentioned as a criterion already in the tender notice.
Summary
In view of the high risk of manipulation in the selection of the winning tenderer in restricted procurement procedures, both countries have introduced special measures to prevent unlawful restrictions on competition.
German rules limit such opportunities for manipulation by means of so-called cross-checking (the four-eyes principle), by maintaining the confidentiality of the list of tenderers and by prohibiting external companies from making proposals for the selection of tenderers.
The Czech rules aim to prevent potential restrictions with a view to manipulation mainly by an unlimited number of tenderers.
However, the audit found that some of the above rules were not applied to a sufficient degree and that an unlawful restriction of the number of candidates was not prevented in some cases.
Contracting authorities are obliged to eliminate the aforementioned shortcomings completely.
Submission of tenders
The stage of the procurement procedure, at which potential tenderers can prepare tenders on the basis of the tender documents they have received, starts with the publication of the tender information and/or the invitation to tenderers.
Until the date of opening the envelopes, potential tenderers can submit their tenders to the contracting entity in order to compete for the award of the contract.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
EU law does not prescribe specific requirements at this stage of the procurement procedure.
The contracting entity must follow the national rules governing this stage of the procedure and prevent potential manipulation.
These rules relate in particular to organisation and control.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
The principle is that tender documents on the basis of which potential suppliers prepare their tenders must always be sent by the contracting entity.
The design offices that are involved in the procedure should not fulfil this role.
The contracting authority must ensure that all candidates have received the same information during this stage of the procurement procedure.
In case of ambiguity in the tender documents or when other questions arise, only the contracting authority may supply the information to the tenderers.
No information may be provided by the design bureaux in charge of drawing up the tender documents.
During this entire stage, any contact between the design bureau and the tenderers must be avoided.
They must remain anonymous throughout, as allowing them to be contacted may adversely affect competition.
Tenders sent by post or submitted directly must be submitted in a closed envelope.
Tenders submitted electronically must be encrypted.
Different provisions
In Germany, the contracting authority may entrust insured external service suppliers with the printing and distribution of the documents, and their employees must be bound to confidentiality.
In Germany, tenders received must be marked with the date of receipt and transmitted directly and immediately in their original state (sealed) to the official in charge of their safekeeping.
This official must have no interest in the procurement procedure.
Tenders received must be locked until the opening date of the envelopes.
In the Czech Republic, tenders received are marked with a serial number.
The contracting authority issues a report with details of the competition, including the tender number and information on the preparation of the tender and its receipt.
There are no specific provisions on the safekeeping of tenders.
Main control findings
BRH
BRH inspected the award of the administrative building.
Sending the procurement documents
The inspected contracting authorities sent the procurement documents either directly or entrusted its printing and distribution to an external firm.
Self-employed persons did not send any documentation.
Information to tenderers
For the part of the tender documents related to questioning and obtaining information, the contracting authority did not follow the rule for separate planning from the tender procedure.
Contrary to the relevant regulations, it often did not mention in the documentation the names of its contact staff, but rather the staff of the project management department.
Some notices during the tender procedure even included the names of companies or self-employed persons who participated in the preparation of the tender documents.
In rare cases, tenderers who asked questions during the tender procedure did not receive identical information.
Labelling of tenders
For the contracting authorities, all accepted tenders were marked with a stamp and a receipt.
However, in some cases for the negotiated procedure, tenders were accepted by self-employed professionals, which violated the rule of one building preventing corruption.
Custody of the tenders
Once marked, not all tenders were passed on directly and without delay to the official assigned to keep them in custody locked the tenders in a steel cabinet.
For one contracting authority, tenders were temporarily placed in an open slot in the mail room, to which all employees had access.
Another contracting authority placed the box in the corridor without constant supervision, and a part of the tenders thrown into the box remained there overnight.
The opening of the box was wide and unsecured, and it was accessible not only to employees.
SAO
The SAO did not find any shortcomings in this area.
Summary
In both countries, rules are laid down to prevent contacts, especially between interested companies and design offices cooperating in the procurement procedure, throughout the entire process.
Equal treatment is ensured by sending the same information to all tenderers.
Rules to prevent manipulation and corruption are suitable for the receipt, safekeeping and confidentiality of tenders.
In Germany, the relevant requirements are somewhat more elaborated than in the Czech Republic.
In Germany, accumulated control records have shown breaches of the rules in some areas.
These irregularities can easily be corrected.
Opening of envelopes
Opening of envelopes
During the opening of envelopes, tenders that have so far been closed are opened, their contents read and published.
The period during which a tenderer is bound by his tender begins when the deadline for submitting or opening envelopes expires and ends with a deadline specified by the contracting authority.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
European legislation does not contain any specific provisions dealing with the opening of envelopes.
However, national laws and regulations contain extensive requirements to protect tenders as well as competition during this sensitive phase of the entire procurement procedure.
The principles of "more eyes", transparency and trustworthiness of the process of opening envelopes are intended to prevent manipulation and modification of tenders already submitted.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Opening of envelopes must be carried out by several persons appointed by the contracting authority.
Compliance with the principle of ‘multiple eyes’ is intended to prevent individuals from covertly manipulating bids.
Bidders may be present when opening envelopes.
When opening, all envelopes must be placed before the chairman of the board and are opened one by one.
Bids received late must not be taken into account.
To ensure transparency when opening envelopes, this stage, including the conclusions (results), must be recorded in writing. The record must include, among other things, the names of the bidders and the price offered.
The record must be read and signed by the group leader.
The record may also be signed by the bidders present.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, a board is set up for opening envelopes.
The participation of independent experts is permitted.
Members of the board may also be entrusted with deliberations during the subsequent stages of the procedure, i.e. the evaluation of the bids.
In Germany, the opening of envelopes takes place in the presence of the manager and the registrar.
The manager must be an employee of the contracting authority.
The manager and, in the case of building, the registrar may not participate in the preparatory stage (preparation of the tender documents), may not decide on the award of the contract and may not be a member of the subsequent processing (management) of the contract.
The German regulations require that the minutes of the opening of envelopes contain not only the tender prices, but also all information on discounts and special (unusual) signs, e.g. damaged envelopes on receipt or late receipt of a tender.
In the Czech Republic, the minutes drawn up must include, in addition to the tender prices, an indication of the tenderer's language and signature authorization.
The German regulations require all parts of the opening envelopes to be marked so as to prevent subsequent changes.
Tenders, usually marked by a form of perforation with a date, must be kept confidential (secret) after the opening of the envelopes has been completed and may only be made available to persons who further process them.
There are no similar arrangements in the Czech Republic.
If a single tender is received in the Czech Republic, the envelope may not be opened and the tenderer must be informed immediately.
In Germany, they do not have this arrangement and if a single tender is accepted, it is opened and evaluated.
Main control findings
BRH
Cross-checking principle
During the opening of the envelopes, the contracting authorities followed the four-eyes principle.
The manager and the registrar, who was not assigned a different role, were always present.
In rare cases, however, for small negotiated procedures, natural persons opened the tenders sent to them without the participation of the contracting authority.
Opening process
The notes from the opening of the envelopes did not always document the progress.
In some cases, tenders submitted late were not marked and in some cases signatures were missing.
Marking of received tenders
The contracting authorities marked the tenders with a date-stamped perforation, which was in line with the requirements.
Some of them had wrongly marked the tenders in the past, but changed their practice after being warned by the BRH.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
SAO
Deadline for the delivery of tenders
During road repairs, for one action, the tenderer's tender was submitted to the contracting authority with a month's delay, which nevertheless accepted it.
Summary
The essential parts of the provisions for opening envelopes are identical in both countries with regard to the principle of "more eyes" and the principle of transparency, thus allowing for a substantial reduction of the risk of corruption and manipulation.
The German rules are more comprehensive than those of the Czech Republic, especially the requirements for accurate documentation of the procedure.
Marking, safe keeping and respecting the confidential nature of tenders substantially limit the possibility of subsequent changes in submitted tenders.
Moreover, the staff responsible for opening envelopes may not carry out any other activities related to the contract to be awarded.
The findings, despite the discovery of some irregularities, confirm that the rules for opening envelopes are largely applied.
Assessment and evaluation of tenders
The contracting authority must examine and evaluate the tenders received within the deadline set.
At the same time, it must award the contract to the tenderer who best meets the award criteria in the tender.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
EU law sets out the process of assessing and evaluating tenders in several steps:
The first step is to check the suitability of the tenderers.
They must be in a good economic and financial position and possess the required professional and technical knowledge and skills.
Bidders who have ever been finally convicted of bribery or defrauding must always be excluded from procurement procedures.
The tenders of non-excluded tenderers must be evaluated in terms of numerical accuracy, technical suitability and value for money on the basis of previously published award criteria.
The contracting authority must identify the economically most favourable tender and, in particular, examine all significantly low price tenders.
Review and evaluation are particularly sensitive and prone to manipulation.
For example, tenderers may be excluded without legitimate reason, or tenders that do not meet the requirements may be left to tender.
The outcome of the evaluation may be improperly influenced by the preference of a tenderer or its disadvantage, thereby preventing the selection of the most economically advantageous tender.
The contracting authority must therefore take special measures in selecting the persons involved in the process and must ensure that they meet the requirements of integrity and technical knowledge.
It must also ensure that the whole process and the decisions taken are transparent and credible.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
In line with EU requirements, the relevant provisions in both countries require the examination and evaluation of tenders in successive stages.
During the evaluation, the requirements for the proof of the suitability of tenderers, the evaluation criteria and their weightings set out in the notice or in the procurement documents may not be changed.
The criteria set for evaluating the suitability of tenderers may no longer be used.
Further evaluation may be carried out only on the basis of evaluation criteria.
The tenders submitted must first be examined from a formal point of view, whether they are complete or do not contain any suspicious elements which might encourage manipulation.
If tenderers are excluded from further examination and evaluation of the tenders submitted by them, they must be informed immediately.
If the evaluation of the tenders results in the discovery of an unreasonably low tender price, the tenderer concerned must be given the opportunity to comment on the situation before his tender may be eliminated.
The reasons for the decision must be documented in a transparent manner.
Different provisions
In Germany, tenderers must maintain confidentiality and only authorised persons may participate in the evaluation of the tenders.
Only the contracting authority may examine the mathematical accuracy of the tenders.
Only employees who are not involved in the decision-making stage of the selection of the contractor or in the implementation of the project may be entrusted with evaluation.
There are no precise provisions as to who may carry out further reviews and evaluations.
If external companies are entrusted with the review and evaluation of tenders, they must be aware that the tenders are confidential and respect this honestly.
They may only make proposals for decisions to the contracting authority.
In the Czech Republic, tenders must be examined and evaluated by a committee appointed by the contracting authority, which draws up a report with a proposal for conclusion of the contract.
However, in both countries, the final decision depends on the contracting authority.
If in Germany the documents and declarations required in connection with the submission of tenders are incomplete, the tenderers concerned must subsequently submit the required documents within six days.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
If tenderers fail to comply with this obligation, their tenders must be excluded.
In the Czech Republic, the contracting authority may decide whether to give the tenderer the opportunity to complete the missing documents.
If the tenderer fails to provide evidence, he must be excluded.
In Germany, not only the total price but also the unit prices must be reviewed.
If the price structure of the tender indicates that prices for parts of the overall project have been estimated to be unreasonably high or low, tenderers must be asked to provide explanations.
If they fail to provide explanations, and if the contracting authority can demonstrate that the tender contains countervailing prices, such tenders must be eliminated.
This is to prevent tenderers from submitting price calculations on the assumption that substantial quantitative changes will be made to individual parts of the overall project, which may lead to a significant increase in project costs.
In the Czech Republic, the total price of a work is evaluated and the prices of individual parts are not examined in detail.
Valuation compensations do not justify the exclusion of a tender.
In Germany, a contract cannot be awarded to a tenderer who offers a price that is unreasonably high or low.
If one or more tenders differ in price by 10 % or more from the price estimated by the contracting authority or from other tenders, tenderers must prove that their tender has been credibly and convincingly calculated.
If they are unable to do so, the contracting authority should exclude them.
In the Czech Republic, a procurement procedure may be cancelled after a price review if only bids with disproportionately low prices have been received and the bidders have not justified these prices.
If the price of the bid is significantly higher than the contracting authority's estimate, this does not constitute a reason for refusing to award the contract to the bidder concerned (unlike German procurement law).
In Germany, if there is a suspicion of behaviour intended to restrict competition, the contracting authority is obliged to report this immediately to the competent superior authority.
The latter must decide on the way forward and whether it is appropriate to inform the antitrust authority or the law enforcement authorities.
Furthermore, it must approve (e.g. if the contract exceeds €50,000 and the contract has not been awarded to the lowest bidder) whether the contract will be awarded in the negotiation of the contract or whether the procurement procedure will be cancelled.
Czech rules do not regulate this area.
Main control findings
BRH
Examination of suitability and award criteria
Most of the procurement procedures examined were in line with the relevant requirements.
In the case of some recommendations made by potential contractors, made by self-employed persons, the contracting authorities did not themselves check the suitability of the bidders.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
In some procurement procedures, insufficient documentation was provided as to whether, in making the choice, the contracting authority took into account all published evaluation criteria or whether the selection decision was based solely on the price criterion.
Selection of interested persons and confidentiality of tenders
Some contracting authorities used the services of unauthorised persons, such as self-employed persons or employees of building authorities.
They were integrated into the planning or implementation process of construction projects, performing functions that were to be performed exclusively by the contracting authority.
The contracting authorities tried to justify this by their insufficient human resources in the sector.
In some cases, they used self-employed professionals to examine the tenders for the services of persons who were not formally bound to preserve the confidentiality of the tenders and to perform with due diligence their obligations under the contract.
As for contracting entities, there was generally no conclusive record of whether tenders in the safekeeping of self-employed professionals were only available to designated persons.
Examination and mathematical control of tenders
Some contracting entities passed open envelopes for further examination to self-employed professionals without first carrying out the prescribed examination of tenders and mathematical examination and without documenting the results of these examinations.
In the event of any intention of manipulation, third parties could make changes to tenders that could not be identified by the contracting entity.
Examination of documents revealed a number of cases where unusual elements in tenders were not adequately identified or where data on prices and products were missing.
Performance audits
A sample check by one contracting authority revealed that in 75% of procurement procedures the most advantageous tenders deviated by more than 10% from the pre-estimated costs.
One in twenty procurement procedures was cancelled due to inadequate tender prices.
In many cases there was a lack of evidence or sufficient documentation on the examination of tenders or on negotiations with tenderers for further clarification.
In many cases there was also a lack of detailed information on, for example, unit prices of the most important invoiced items.
Changes in market prices or increases in material costs were often cited as reasons for deviations.
Contracting authorities argued that identifying and demonstrating balancing prices was difficult.
In the case of one call for tenders, made by an entity outside the ordinary contracting authorities, a price 66% lower than the original cost estimate was offered.
No details were recorded on the closer examination of cost or on the reasons for deviations.
In clarifying this issue, the contracting authority found no indication of price dumping in the bidder to whom the contract might have been awarded.
It was therefore not clarified whether the original cost estimate was excessive or whether the price offered was too low.
Later, the contracting authority rated this bidder as a "low-cost company" which was unable to meet either the quality requirements or the deadlines.
In another procurement procedure, the contracting authority awarded the contract to a bidder whose price was 35% higher than the original cost estimate.
The contracting authority did not cancel the procurement procedure and argued that the said bidder could be assumed not to have based its pricing on unreasonably high costs.
Suspected irregularities
Two cases of irregularities were found in one contracting authority and these were reported to the firm responsible for technical supervision.
In one procurement procedure, two bidders submitted bids containing identical price calculations, which prompted the contracting authority to exclude both bids.
The audits did not show any significant deficiencies in the application of other provisions.
SAO
Assessment of suitability
In many construction projects, such as university buildings, roads and railways, the contracting authorities did not exclude bidders from participation in the procurement procedure, even though they did not meet the requirements of the contracting authority specified in the tender specifications, or did not substantiate the qualification to the required extent, or, on the contrary, one contracting authority failed to substantiate or explain the reason for the exclusion of the bidder who met the requirements.
Evaluation
In the case of the construction of a wastewater treatment plant, individual evaluation criteria of the procurement procedure for the construction contractor were established, but the contracting authority did not respect them during the procedure.
In the case of the railway infrastructure development project, the weightings of the evaluation criteria were not determined at the time of the contract award, but only at the beginning of the evaluation.
In the case of the infrastructure repair project, the evaluation of the criterion “shortening the construction time” was not carried out correctly.
For the bidders in second and third place, the evaluation incorrectly indicated a greater shortening of construction time than indicated in their bids.
Documentation of the reasons for the selection of the bidder
For the three housing promotion actions, the contracting authority did not substantiate the criteria on which it had assessed the bids received and how it had selected the most suitable one.
The correctness of the selection of small-scale contractors associated with the construction of a road ring with an estimated price of up to CZK 500,000.
It was not possible to verify the correctness of the selection of the contractors, as the contractors were selected only on the basis of telephone consultations on price and timing of performance, but evidence of these negotiations was not produced.
Summary
In both countries, the rules for the verification and evaluation of bids correspond to EU requirements.
However, they differ with regard to the specific steps of the procedure and the selection of individuals involved in the procedure.
Unlike in the Czech Republic, in Germany, these steps of the procurement process are governed by stricter and more detailed requirements, in particular as regards the application of the cross-principle and the protection of bids.
The same applies to the rules for the verification of the appropriateness of the prices offered.
In both countries, in some cases checks found that the substantive steps of the process in the verification and evaluation of tenders were not documented at all and, if so, only partially.
The absence of control tools and transparency in the decision-making during the examination and evaluation of tenders allows for manipulation.
Checks showed serious deficiencies, such as the transmission of tenders to third parties without prior due diligence and the documentation of tenders by the contracting authority.
If contracting authorities entrust a third party with the performance of functions related to the examination and evaluation of tenders, appropriate measures and increased control of measures against the increased possibility of manipulation are necessary.
When checking the validity of prices, the German contracting authorities audited did not in all cases use the full set of tools available and did not deal with their own price structure.
In the Czech Republic, deficiencies were found in the evaluation of the appropriateness of tenders and the application of evaluation criteria.
Completion of the procurement procedure
After the examination and evaluation of the tenders, the procurement process is concluded by the award of the contract or the cancellation of the procedure.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
In the case of the European procedure, all tenderers must be informed in advance of the decision to award the contract.
Contractors have the opportunity to review the decision taken within a limited period of 10 or 15 days and to request its review if appropriate.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
The period of 10 days applies to electronic submissions and 15 days to other submissions.
The contract may not be concluded before the expiry of this period or before the decision of the review procedure.
If the review body rejects the complaint and confirms the decision, candidates and tenderers must be given the opportunity to have the correctness of the application of the procurement law reviewed by an independent court.
This measure ensures that no contracting authority will be able to circumvent the legal protection of suppliers by swiftly implementing the decision of the procurement procedure.
The contracting authority must document the progress of the procedure appropriately.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have implemented the procedure on information obligations.
In both countries, the final decision on the outcome of the procurement procedure rests with the contracting authority.
However, the latter cannot arbitrarily deviate from the results of a properly performed screening and evaluation.
The contracting authority is to conclude the contract within a specified period.
This is because the tenderer is not bound by its bid after this period has expired.
The content of the contract must not deviate from the winning bid.
Unsuccessful tenderers are to be informed of the result in a timely manner, stating the reason why their bid was not accepted.
Cancellation of the procurement procedure is permissible only if certain conditions are met, e.g. if the prices in the tenders do not correspond to the specifications.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the mandatory waiting period prior to the conclusion of the contract of 10 or 15 days applies to both European and national procedures.
In Germany, the equivalent provision applies only to European procedures.
In all other procedures in Germany, tenderers cannot be informed of the award of the contract before the decision on its EU provision and the review award is taken.
In the Czech Republic, a procurement procedure must be cancelled if a single tender has been received or if only one tender meets the requirements for its consideration and evaluation.
In Germany, a contract must be awarded even if a single tender has been received, provided that the tender fulfils the conditions set out in the tender documentation and is cost-effective.
Main audit findings
BRH
The audits found few irregularities in the completion of the audited procurement processes.
In some cases of European procedures, companies receiving letters of authority before the expiry of the notification period, which may invalidate the contract.
Also in several cases of open procedures, letters of authority contained modifications incompatible with the content of the tender, e.g. new deadlines.
In some cases, contracting authorities did not meet the deadlines for awarding the contract.
In one open procedure, the contracting authority selected the second most advantageous offer because a bidder offering a price more than 50% lower no longer wished to fulfill the contract at the price originally offered.
SAO
In one case, during the reconstruction and construction of a university, the contracting authority did not conclude a contract with the contractor in accordance with the contract proposal contained in the selected bidder's bid.
In some projects for repairs and maintenance of roads, construction of a motorway and a road ring, contracts were concluded later than stipulated by law.
In the case of construction modifications in former military districts, the contracting authority sent a notice of selection of the most suitable offer to all bidders after the legal deadline had expired.
Summary
Both countries implemented EU regulations on the termination of procurement procedures.
Legal protection of unsuccessful candidates contributes to reducing corruption.
The Czech Public Procurement Act includes the institute of legal protection even in cases of contracts below the EU limits.
In both countries, it was found that the limited time limit for concluding the contract was exceeded.
This can cause adverse economic consequences and increase the risk of manipulation.
In the future, contracting entities should eliminate such shortcomings when concluding procurement procedures.
Documentation, notifications, information obligations
The procurement process, with all its individual stages, relevant findings and decisions, including relevant justifications, must be documented in a transparent manner.
The completion of procurement procedures at European level must be published.
The data on procurement procedures are summarised in EU statistics.
Requirements of European law and prevention of corruption
The obligation to document procurement procedures complies with the fundamental requirement of the principle of transparency, applied at EU-wide level.
Only proper and time-relevant documentation on decision-making and justification gives a guarantee of transparency in the procurement process and, above all, guarantees effective legal protection of the interests of each bidder in cases where the value of the contract exceeds European thresholds.
Adequate documentation is also an essential part of anti-corruption measures, as the obligation to provide detailed reasons for individual decisions and their merits significantly reduces the scope for manipulation.
In European procurement procedures, the contracting authority must notify the completion of the procedures to the EU's Office of Official Publications within 48 days from the date of the award of the contract.
The Office publishes the notification in the Official Journal of the EU and this also fulfils the requirement of transparency.
The contracting authority must summarise the essential data on its procurement procedures and communicate them to the European Commission through the relevant national ministries.
On this basis, the European Commission issues annual statistics which provide an overview of the development of the single market in the field of construction.
Provisions of German and Czech procurement law
Same or similar provisions
Both countries have fully transposed into their national legislation the provisions of the European law on documentation and notification obligations.
The provisions on documentation and notification for the purposes of European statistics are almost identical in both countries.
Different provisions
In the Czech Republic, the obligation to notify the award of a contract applies not only to European procedures, but also to national procurement procedures.
The deadline for notification is only 15 days from the conclusion of the contract.
In Germany, the obligation to notify procurement procedures, in addition to those which are mandatory under European law, applies only to restricted procedures exceeding the limit of €25,000 ($625,000) and direct procedures exceeding the limit of €15,000 ($375,000) including VAT.
Below this limit, only the participants in the procedure are informed.
Main control findings
BRH
Documentation
A large number of awards were not fully documented.
The notice did not contain all the necessary information, e.g. reasons for the supply specifications and national rules.
In the case of one procedure, the contracting authority did not explain why it concluded a contract with a tenderer whose tender did not contain the mandatory elements.
In several cases, some required records were also missing.
Publication of the award
In some European procedures, the contracting authority made errors in the publication of the notice.
In several awards, evidence of the publication of the notice was missing.
Reporting obligation
Statistically verified files included the required minimum of information and were created and sent on time.
Preliminary transparency
In most cases, the contracting authority published information on the outcome of the contract.
In the case of one contracting authority, information on the contract was not published due to a communication error.
It took the contracting authority half a year to notice the error.
As a result, the outcome of the procedure was not published during this time.
SAO
Documentation
In events related to the organisation of a major sporting event, significant shortcomings in the documentation of the procurement procedure were found, such as incorrect data on bid prices in the reports of the evaluation committee.
In addition, some documents on the opening of envelopes were not signed.
Publication of the award
In the preparation of repairs and maintenance of roads, construction of a motorway and a road ring, the notices of the award were sent after the deadline, or were not published.
Summary
The provisions on documentation and notification and notification obligations include European requirements and are almost identical in both countries.
This generally ensures transparency of procurement procedures and final selection.
Czech notification obligations are somewhat stricter compared to German ones.
The audits revealed shortcomings significantly reducing the value of documentation as a means of preventing corruption.
THISsegmentISintentionallyLEFTblank
Contracting entities should continue to strengthen the transparency of their procurement procedures and fully comply with notification and notification requirements.
Summary evaluation and recommendations
The parallel audits of the SAO and BRH focused on two areas that are essential for the public procurement process in both the Czech Republic and Germany, namely the application of EU procurement law and the prevention of corruption.
EU legislation provides an opportunity for international competition within the EU with the aim of creating a single European market.
Measures aimed at preventing corruption are aimed not only at avoiding it or at reducing it, but also at preventing financial damage and potential loss of confidence in the integrity of the government.
The audit findings of the two audit institutions – the SAO and BRH – with regard to the application of both countries' procurement law and a comparison of its respective provisions give an internal view on common and different elements that can help both countries in further development and application of this law.
Application of EU procurement law
Both countries have transposed EU procurement law into their national legislation.
In both countries there is an important common base for public procurement and fulfilment of the basic requirements in the areas of: competition; equal treatment of candidates; transparency of procurement procedures.
Contracting authorities strengthen these requirements partly with very extensive measures.
The Czech Republic has extended public procurement provisions requiring a European procedure to procedures below the EU limits and is therefore governed by national legislation.
Nevertheless, the audits showed that contracting authorities are not always fully compliant with all the relevant provisions of EU requirements.
German contracting authorities should continue to improve the quality of their procurement procedures in order to eliminate the aforementioned irregularities, taking into account the implementation of EU requirements.
In the Czech Republic, the audits identified risks affecting economy and value for money.
These include, in particular, incomplete definition of the subject matter of the public contract, which resulted in additional works that were often awarded in violation of the law, insufficient definition of the anticipated price, and the required qualification conditions reducing the potential pool of bidders and thus reducing competition, especially in the area of transport construction.
Preventing corruption
The joint audits were not aimed at detecting cases of corruption.
The reason was that both BRH and the SAO did not have sufficient tools to do so.
However, they can check whether the competent authorities of both countries take measures to reduce corruption and manipulation in public procurement and how these measures are applied.
As regards the prevention of corruption, the two countries focused on different aspects.
In particular, the German provisions give room for wide and unlimited competition, transparency and controls as a central element of the prevention of corruption.
The Czech regulations aim mainly at enhancing the transparency of public procurement procedures.
BRH
In Germany, there are general principles for the prevention of corruption, which apply to the whole federal administration.
These principles were also included among the requirements for public procurement and were adopted in order to meet the specific needs of public procurement.
The relevant provisions specify, sometimes with great precision, the methods of compliance with the requirements for the prevention of corruption during the procurement procedure and for the selection of persons included in it.
In BRH's view, the principle of preference for open procurement procedures and calls for participation is a particularly suitable tool for substantially reducing the risk of corruption in public procurement, while restricting competition increases the risk of manipulation.
Risk analysis and adequate controls (in particular the principle of cross-checking), transparency requirements, tender documentation and various organisational measures are also effective mechanisms for preventing corruption and manipulation.
BRH therefore does not feel the need for substantial changes to the current provisions that public institutions have to comply with when awarding public contracts.
Anti-corruption measures are widely applied in practice.
Irregularities found in some areas have highlighted weaknesses in compliance with certain requirements.
Therefore, contracting authorities should provide their employees with further training to familiarise them with anti-corruption requirements and further raise their awareness in this area.
A significant part of the revealed weaknesses relate to activities delegated by the building administration to private entrepreneurs.
However, the absence of adequate controls when delegating powers increases the risk of misuse.
Activities susceptible to manipulation and corruption should therefore be transferred to third parties only if the administration can guarantee a sufficient degree of protection against unauthorized interference.
SAO
Anti-corruption strategies were adopted at government level in the Czech Republic, which also significantly affect the area of public procurement.
Audit actions of the SAO took place during the period when one of the most important parts of the strategy was implemented, namely a significant change of the Public Procurement Act and the adoption of subsequent implementing regulations.
The adopted strategy and new legal regulations can be effective steps to improve the situation.
Under the new regulations, the contracting authority must justify the effectiveness of the public contract, the determination of the expected value of the public contract, the adequacy of the requirements for qualification, the determination of basic evaluation criteria and the method of evaluation of tenders, and document and publish everything properly.
Recommendations
Overall, the SAO and BRH have a favourable opinion on the ways of implementing the EU requirements.
Their wide application in practice confirmed that the EU requirements on competition, equal treatment and transparency form an appropriate basis for public procurement within the EU.
The supranational model, established in the EU, can also serve as a basis for similar projects aimed at facilitating and supporting international competition.
Taking into account the great potential of material and intangible damages arising from manipulation and corruption in connection with public procurement, both BRH and SAO insist that it is the obligation of contracting authorities to continue and improve measures against corruption and manipulation where possible.
In particular, organisational structures and processes of procurement procedures should be continuously monitored for weaknesses and risks in order to identify the need for corrective actions and to react appropriately.
Compared to the practice in Germany, it has emerged that the Czech Republic should increase the share of open procurement procedures, which give space to competition and substantially reduce the possibility of corrupt behaviour.
Procurement in parts, where permissible and suitable for contracting authorities, can also have a positive impact on the achieved price. A higher number of bidders puts pressure on the achieved price.
The organisation of the procurement procedure also has an influence on the course of the procurement procedure.
In Germany, it is organised by special state organisations, while in the Czech Republic, with the exception of transport constructions, it is organised by the central authorities themselves or through contracting organisations.
Both the SAO and the BRH wish to further develop knowledge in their work, where appropriate, and to address it with their parliament and government beyond their joint project.
Moreover, they are convinced that the principles of procurement law and their implementation, as described in this report, will also be of interest at the level of the International Organisation of Supreme Audit Institutions (INTOSAI), as the principles and findings mentioned are of general importance for the effective fight against corruption and for ensuring that public contracts are awarded in a regime of well-functioning and honest competition and that value for money is achieved.
